data-language-local-name="Turkish" class="interlanguage-link-target"><span>Türkçe</span></a></li><li class="interlanguage-link interwiki-uk mw-list-item"><a href="https://uk.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%95%D1%85%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%86%D1%96%D1%8F_%D1%82%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BD" title="Ехолокація тварин – Ukrainian" lang="uk" hreflang="uk" data-title="Ехолокація тварин" data-language-autonym="Українська" data-language-local-name="Ukrainian" class="interlanguage-link-target"><span>Українська</span></a></li><li class="interlanguage-link interwiki-zh-yue mw-list-item"><a href="https://zh-yue.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%8F%8D%E9%9F%BF%E5%AE%9A%E4%BD%8D" title="反響定位 – Cantonese" lang="yue" hreflang="yue" data-title="反響定位" data-language-autonym="粵語" data-language-local-name="Cantonese" class="interlanguage-link-target"><span>粵語</span></a></li><li class="interlanguage-link interwiki-zh mw-list-item"><a href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%9B%9E%E5%A3%B0%E5%AE%9A%E4%BD%8D" title="回声定位 – Chinese" lang="zh" hreflang="zh" data-title="回声定位" data-language-autonym="中文" data-language-local-name="Chinese" class="interlanguage-link-target"><span>中文</span></a></li>
			</ul>
			<div class="after-portlet after-portlet-lang"><span class="wb-langlinks-edit wb-langlinks-link"><a href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Special:EntityPage/Q6921783#sitelinks-wikipedia" title="Edit interlanguage links" class="wbc-editpage">Edit links</a></span></div>
		</div>

	</div>
</div>

				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		<div id="mw-indicator-good-star" class="mw-indicator"><div class="mw-parser-output"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Good_articles*" title="This is a good article. Click here for more information."><img alt="This is a good article. Click here for more information." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/20px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png" decoding="async" width="19" height="20" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/40px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="185" /></a></span></div></div>
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Method used by several animal species to determine location using sound</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Animal_echolocation.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Animal_echolocation.svg/500px-Animal_echolocation.svg.png" decoding="async" width="450" height="347" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Animal_echolocation.svg/960px-Animal_echolocation.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="318" data-file-height="245" /></a><figcaption>A depiction of the ultrasound signals emitted by a bat, and the echo from a nearby object</figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Echolocation</b>, also called <b>bio sonar</b>, is a biological <a href="/wiki/Active_sonar" class="mw-redirect" title="Active sonar">active sonar</a> used by several <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> groups, both in the air and underwater. Echolocating animals emit calls and listen to the <a href="/wiki/Echo_(phenomenon)" class="mw-redirect" title="Echo (phenomenon)">echoes</a> of those calls that return from various objects near them. They use these echoes to locate and identify the objects. Echolocation is used for <a href="/wiki/Animal_navigation" title="Animal navigation">navigation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foraging" title="Foraging">foraging</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">hunting prey</a>.
</p><p>Echolocation calls can be <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">frequency modulated</a> (FM, varying in pitch during the call) or constant frequency (CF). FM offers precise range discrimination to localize the prey, at the cost of reduced operational range. CF allows both the prey's velocity and its movements to be detected by means of the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler effect</a>. FM may be best for close, cluttered environments, while CF may be better in open environments or for hunting while perched.
</p><p>Echolocating animals include <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Odontocetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Odontocetes">odontocetes</a> (toothed whales) and some <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bat</a> species, and, using simpler forms, species in other groups such as <a href="/wiki/Shrew" title="Shrew">shrews</a>. A few bird species in two cave-dwelling bird groups echolocate, namely <a href="/wiki/Cave_swiftlet" title="Cave swiftlet">cave swiftlets</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Oilbird" title="Oilbird">oilbird</a>.
</p><p>Some prey animals that are hunted by echolocating bats take <a href="/wiki/Anti-predator_adaptation" title="Anti-predator adaptation">active countermeasures</a> to avoid capture. These include predator avoidance, attack deflection, and the use of <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasonic</a> clicks, which have evolved multiple functions including <a href="/wiki/Aposematism" title="Aposematism">aposematism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Batesian_mimicry" title="Batesian mimicry">mimicry of chemically defended species</a>, and echolocation jamming.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Early_research">Early research</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early research"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The term <i>echolocation</i> had been coined by 1944 by the American zoologist <a href="/wiki/Donald_Griffin" title="Donald Griffin">Donald Griffin</a>, who, with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Galambos" title="Robert Galambos">Robert Galambos</a>, first demonstrated the phenomenon in bats.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Griffin&#95;1944&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffin_1944-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As Griffin described in his book,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the 18th century Italian scientist <a href="/wiki/Lazzaro_Spallanzani" title="Lazzaro Spallanzani">Lazzaro Spallanzani</a> had, by means of a series of elaborate experiments, concluded that when bats fly at night, they rely on some sense besides vision, but he did not discover that the other sense was hearing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Swiss physician and naturalist <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jurine" title="Louis Jurine">Louis Jurine</a> repeated Spallanzani's experiments (using different species of bat), and concluded in 1798 that when bats hunt at night, they rely on hearing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1908, Walter Louis Hahn confirmed Spallanzani's and Jurine's findings.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1912, the inventor <a href="/wiki/Hiram_Maxim" title="Hiram Maxim">Hiram Maxim</a> independently proposed that bats used <a href="/wiki/Infrasound" title="Infrasound">sound below the human auditory range</a> to avoid obstacles.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1920, the English physiologist <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Hartridge" title="Hamilton Hartridge">Hamilton Hartridge</a> correctly proposed instead that bats used <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">frequencies above the range of human hearing</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Echolocation in <a href="/wiki/Odontocetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Odontocetes">odontocetes</a> (toothed whales) was not properly described until two decades after Griffin and Galambos' work, by <a href="/wiki/William_E._Schevill" title="William E. Schevill">Schevill</a> and McBride in 1956.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, in 1953, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Yves_Cousteau" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacques Yves Cousteau">Jacques Yves Cousteau</a> suggested in his first book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Silent_World:_A_Story_of_Undersea_Discovery_and_Adventure" title="The Silent World: A Story of Undersea Discovery and Adventure">The Silent World</a></i>, that porpoises had something like <a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">sonar</a>, judging by their navigational abilities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Principles">Principles</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Principles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Echolocation is active <a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">sonar</a>, using sounds made by the animal itself. Ranging is achieved by measuring the time delay between the animal's own sound emission and any echoes that return from the environment. The relative intensity of sound received at each ear, as well as the time delay between arrival at the two ears, provide information about the horizontal angle (azimuth) from which the reflected sound waves arrive.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike some human-made sonars that rely on many extremely narrow beams and many receivers to localize a target (<a href="/wiki/Multibeam_sonar" class="mw-redirect" title="Multibeam sonar">multibeam sonar</a>), animal echolocation has only one transmitter and two receivers (the ears) positioned slightly apart. The echoes returning to the ears arrive at different times and at different intensities, depending on the position of the object generating the echoes. The time and loudness differences are used by the animals to perceive distance and direction. With echolocation, the bat or other animal can tell, not only where it is going, but also how big another animal is, what kind of animal it is, and other features.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Acoustic_features">Acoustic features</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Acoustic features"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Describing the diversity of echolocation calls requires examination of the frequency and temporal features of the calls. It is the variations in these aspects that produce echolocation calls suited for different acoustic environments and hunting behaviors. The calls of bats have been most intensively researched, but the principles apply to all echolocation calls.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Thaler&#95;Goodale&#95;2016&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thaler_Goodale_2016-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hiryu&#95;2007&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hiryu_2007-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bat call frequencies range from as low as 11&#160;kHz to as high as 212&#160;kHz.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jones,&#95;G.&#95;2007&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones,_G._2007-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">Insectivorous</a> aerial-hawking bats, those that chase prey in the open air, have a call <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> between 20&#160;kHz and 60&#160;kHz, because it is the frequency that gives the best range and image acuity and makes them less conspicuous to insects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, low frequencies are adaptive for some species with different prey and environments. <i><a href="/wiki/Euderma_maculatum" class="mw-redirect" title="Euderma maculatum">Euderma maculatum</a></i>, a bat species that feeds on <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moths</a>, uses a particularly low frequency of 12.7&#160;kHz that cannot be heard by moths.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fullard&#95;1997&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fullard_1997-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Echolocation calls can be composed of two different types of frequency structure: <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulated" class="mw-redirect" title="Frequency modulated">frequency modulated</a> (FM) sweeps, and constant frequency (CF) tones. A particular call can consist of one, the other, or both structures. An FM sweep is a broadband signal – that is, it contains a downward sweep through a range of frequencies. A CF tone is a narrowband signal: the sound stays constant at one frequency throughout its duration.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Echolocation calls in bats have been measured at intensities anywhere between 60 and 140 <a href="/wiki/Decibels" class="mw-redirect" title="Decibels">decibels</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Certain bat species can modify their call intensity mid-call, lowering the intensity as they approach objects that reflect sound strongly. This prevents the returning echo from deafening the bat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hiryu&#95;2007&#95;19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hiryu_2007-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> High-intensity calls such as those from aerial-hawking bats (133&#160;dB) are adaptive to hunting in open skies. Their high intensity calls are necessary to even have moderate detection of surroundings because air has a high absorption of ultrasound and because insects' size only provide a small target for sound reflection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, the so-called "whispering bats" have adapted low-amplitude echolocation so that their prey, moths, which are able to hear echolocation calls, are less able to detect and avoid an oncoming bat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fullard&#95;1997&#95;22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fullard_1997-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Brinklov&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brinklov-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A single echolocation call (a call being a single continuous trace on a sound <a href="/wiki/Spectrogram" title="Spectrogram">spectrogram</a>, and a series of calls comprising a sequence or pass) can last anywhere from less than 3 to over 50 milliseconds in duration. Pulse duration is around 3 milliseconds in FM bats such as <a href="/wiki/Phyllostomidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Phyllostomidae">Phyllostomidae</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Vespertilionidae" title="Vespertilionidae">Vespertilionidae</a>; between 7 and 16 milliseconds in Quasi-constant-frequency (QCF) bats such as other Vespertilionidae, <a href="/wiki/Emballonuridae" title="Emballonuridae">Emballonuridae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Molossidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Molossidae">Molossidae</a>; and between 11 milliseconds (<a href="/wiki/Hipposideridae" title="Hipposideridae">Hipposideridae</a>) and 52 milliseconds (<a href="/wiki/Rhinolophidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhinolophidae">Rhinolophidae</a>) in CF bats.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jones&#95;1999&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_1999-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Duration depends also on the stage of prey-catching behavior that the bat is engaged in, usually decreasing when the bat is in the final stages of prey capture – this enables the bat to call more rapidly without overlap of call and echo. Reducing duration comes at the cost of having less total sound available for reflecting off objects and being heard by the bat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jones,&#95;G.&#95;2007&#95;20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones,_G._2007-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The time interval between subsequent echolocation calls (or pulses) determines two aspects of a bat's perception. First, it establishes how quickly the bat's auditory scene information is updated. For example, bats increase the repetition rate of their calls (that is, decrease the pulse interval) as they home in on a target. This allows the bat to get new information regarding the target's location at a faster rate when it needs it most. Secondly, the pulse interval determines the maximum range that bats can detect objects. This is because bats can only keep track of the echoes from one call at a time; as soon as they make another call they stop listening for echoes from the previously made call. For example, a pulse interval of 100 ms (typical of a bat searching for insects) allows sound to travel in air roughly 34 meters so a bat can only detect objects as far away as 17 meters (the sound has to travel out and back). With a pulse interval of 5 ms (typical of a bat in the final moments of a capture attempt), the bat can only detect objects up to 85&#160;cm away. Therefore, the bat constantly has to make a choice between getting new information updated quickly and detecting objects far away.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Tradeoff_between_FM_and_CF">Tradeoff between FM and CF</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Tradeoff between FM and CF"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="FM_signal_advantages">FM signal advantages</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: FM signal advantages"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" style="width:250px;" data-durationhint="56" data-mwtitle="Yannick_Dauby_-_Bats_echolocation_(CC_by).ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Yannick_Dauby_-_Bats_echolocation_(CC_by).ogg"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b1/Yannick_Dauby_-_Bats_echolocation_%28CC_by%29.ogg/Yannick_Dauby_-_Bats_echolocation_%28CC_by%29.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b1/Yannick_Dauby_-_Bats_echolocation_%28CC_by%29.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span><figcaption>Echolocation call produced by <i><a href="/wiki/Pipistrellus_pipistrellus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pipistrellus pipistrellus">Pipistrellus pipistrellus</a></i>, an FM bat. The ultrasonic call has been "<a href="/wiki/Heterodyne" title="Heterodyne">heterodyned</a>" – multiplied by a constant frequency to produce frequency subtraction, and thus an audible sound – by a bat detector. A key feature of the recording is the increase in the repetition rate of the call as the bat nears its target – this is called the "terminal buzz".</figcaption></figure>
<p>The major advantage conferred by an FM signal is extremely precise range discrimination, or <a href="/wiki/Sound_localization" title="Sound localization">localization</a>, of the target. J. A. Simmons demonstrated this effect with a series of experiments that showed how bats using FM signals could distinguish between two separate targets even when the targets were less than half a millimeter apart. This ability is due to the broadband sweep of the signal, which allows for better resolution of the time delay between the call and the returning echo, thereby improving the cross correlation of the two. If harmonic frequencies are added to the FM signal, then this localization becomes even more precise.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jones&#95;2006&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_2006-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Grinnell&#95;1995&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grinnell_1995-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Simmons&#95;1980&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simmons_1980-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One possible disadvantage of the FM signal is a decreased operational range of the call. Because the energy of the call is spread out among many frequencies, the distance at which the FM-bat can detect targets is limited.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fenton&#95;1995&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fenton_1995-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is in part because any echo returning at a particular frequency can only be evaluated for a brief fraction of a millisecond, as the fast downward sweep of the call does not remain at any one frequency for long.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Grinnell&#95;1995&#95;30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grinnell_1995-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="CF_signal_advantages">CF signal advantages</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: CF signal advantages"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The structure of a CF signal is adaptive in that it allows the CF-bat to detect both the velocity of a target, and the fluttering of a target's wings as Doppler shifted frequencies. A <a href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" class="mw-redirect" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> is an alteration in sound wave frequency, and is produced in two relevant situations: when the bat and its target are moving relative to each other, and when the target's wings are oscillating back and forth. CF-bats must compensate for Doppler shifts, lowering the frequency of their call in response to echoes of elevated frequency – this ensures that the returning echo remains at the frequency to which the ears of the bat are most finely tuned. The oscillation of a target's wings also produces amplitude shifts, which gives a CF-bat additional help in distinguishing a flying target from a stationary one.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Neuweiler&#95;2003&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neuweiler_2003-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jones&#95;2006&#95;29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_2006-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The horseshoe bats hunt in this way.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schnitzler&#95;Flieger&#95;1983&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schnitzler_Flieger_1983-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, because the signal energy of a CF call is concentrated into a narrow frequency band, the operational range of the call is much greater than that of an FM signal. This relies on the fact that echoes returning within the narrow frequency band can be summed over the entire length of the call, which maintains a constant frequency for up to 100 milliseconds.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Grinnell&#95;1995&#95;30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grinnell_1995-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fenton&#95;1995&#95;32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fenton_1995-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Acoustic_environments_of_FM_and_CF_signals">Acoustic environments of FM and CF signals</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Acoustic environments of FM and CF signals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An FM component is excellent for hunting prey while flying in close, cluttered environments. Two aspects of the FM signal account for this fact: the precise target localization conferred by the broadband signal, and the short duration of the call. The first of these is essential because in a cluttered environment, the bats must be able to resolve their prey from large amounts of background noise. The 3D localization abilities of the broadband signal enable the bat to do exactly that, providing it with what Simmons and Stein (1980) call a "clutter rejection strategy".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Simmons&#95;1980&#95;31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simmons_1980-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This strategy is further improved by the use of harmonics, which, as previously stated, enhance the localization properties of the call. The short duration of the FM call is also best in close, cluttered environments because it enables the bat to emit many calls extremely rapidly without overlap. This means that the bat can get an almost continuous stream of information – essential when objects are close, because they will pass by quickly – without confusing which echo corresponds to which call.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Neuweiler&#95;2003&#95;33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neuweiler_2003-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jones&#95;2006&#95;29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_2006-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A CF component is often used by bats hunting for prey while flying in open, clutter-free environments, or by bats that wait on perches for their prey to appear. The success of the former strategy is due to two aspects of the CF call, both of which confer excellent prey-detection abilities. First, the greater working range of the call allows bats to detect targets present at great distances – a common situation in open environments. Second, the length of the call is also suited for targets at great distances: in this case, there is a decreased chance that the long call will overlap with the returning echo. The latter strategy is made possible by the fact that the long, narrowband call allows the bat to detect Doppler shifts, which would be produced by an insect moving either towards or away from a perched bat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Neuweiler&#95;2003&#95;33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neuweiler_2003-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Simmons&#95;1980&#95;31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simmons_1980-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jones&#95;2006&#95;29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_2006-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Taxonomic_range">Taxonomic range</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Taxonomic range"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Echolocation occurs in a variety of mammals and birds as described below.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It evolved repeatedly, an example of <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jones&#95;2006&#95;29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_2006-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Racicot&#95;2019&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Racicot_2019-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Bats">Bats</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bats"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Chirps190918-22s2.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Chirps190918-22s2.png/500px-Chirps190918-22s2.png" decoding="async" width="380" height="49" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Chirps190918-22s2.png/960px-Chirps190918-22s2.png 2x" data-file-width="1816" data-file-height="234" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Spectrogram" title="Spectrogram">Spectrogram</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Pipistrellus_pipistrellus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pipistrellus pipistrellus">Pipistrellus pipistrellus</a></i> bat vocalizations during prey approach. The recording covers a total of 1.1&#160;seconds; lower main frequency c. 45&#160;kHz (as typical for a common pipistrelle). About 150&#160;milliseconds before final contact time between and duration of calls are becoming much shorter ("feeding buzz").<br />Corresponding audio file: <span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" style="width:250px;" data-durationhint="23" data-mwtitle="Chirps190918-22s.mp3" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e5/Chirps190918-22s.mp3/Chirps190918-22s.mp3.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e5/Chirps190918-22s.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span> </figcaption></figure>

<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><figure class="mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/60px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/120px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></span><figcaption></figcaption></figure></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Pipistrellus.ogg" title="File:Pipistrellus.ogg">Pipistrellus calls</a></div>
<div><span typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="232" style="width:232px;" data-durationhint="6" data-mwtitle="Pipistrellus.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/Pipistrellus.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/94/Pipistrellus.ogg/Pipistrellus.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></div>
<div class="description">Recording of <i>Pipistrellus</i>; echolocation call followed by a social call.</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>Echolocating bats use echolocation to <a href="/wiki/Animal_navigation" title="Animal navigation">navigate</a> and forage, often in total darkness. They generally emerge from their roosts in caves, attics, or trees at dusk and hunt for insects into the night. Using echolocation, bats can determine how far away an object is, the object's size, shape and density, and the direction (if any) that an object is moving. Their use of echolocation, along with powered flight, allows them to occupy a niche where there are often many <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> (that come out at night since there are fewer predators then), less competition for food, and fewer species that may prey on the bats themselves.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Echolocating bats generate <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a> and emit the sound through the open mouth or, much more rarely, the nose.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The latter is most pronounced in the <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_bats" class="mw-redirect" title="Horseshoe bats">horseshoe bats</a> (<i>Rhinolophus spp.</i>). Bat echolocation calls range in frequency from 14,000 to well over 100,000&#160;Hz, mostly beyond the range of the human ear (typical human hearing range is considered to be from 20&#160;Hz to 20,000&#160;Hz). Bats may estimate the elevation of targets by interpreting the <a href="/wiki/Interference_pattern" class="mw-redirect" title="Interference pattern">interference patterns</a> caused by the echoes reflecting from the <a href="/wiki/Tragus_(ear)" title="Tragus (ear)">tragus</a>, a flap of skin in the external ear.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Individual bat species echolocate within specific frequency ranges that suit their environment and prey types. This has sometimes been used by researchers to identify bats flying in an area simply by recording their calls with ultrasonic recorders known as "bat detectors". However, echolocation calls are not always species specific and some bats overlap in the type of calls they use so recordings of echolocation calls cannot be used to identify all bats. Researchers in several countries have developed "bat call libraries" that contain "reference call" recordings of local bat species to assist with identification.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>When searching for prey they produce sounds at a low rate (10–20 clicks/second). During the search phase the sound emission is coupled to respiration, which is again coupled to the wingbeat. This coupling appears to dramatically conserve energy as there is little to no additional energetic cost of echolocation to flying bats.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After detecting a potential prey item, echolocating bats increase the rate of pulses, ending with the <b>terminal buzz</b>, at rates as high as 200 clicks/second. During approach to a detected target, the duration of the sounds is gradually decreased, as is the energy of the sound.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Bat_evolution">Bat evolution</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Bat evolution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Bats evolved at the start of the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> epoch, around 64 <a href="/wiki/Myr" class="mw-redirect" title="Myr">mya</a>. The Yangochiroptera appeared some 55 mya, and the Rhinolophoidea some 52 mya.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Teeling&#95;Springer&#95;Madsen&#95;Bates&#95;2005&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Teeling_Springer_Madsen_Bates_2005-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There are two hypotheses about the evolution of echolocation in bats. The first suggests that <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">laryngeal</a> echolocation evolved twice, or more, in Chiroptera, at least once in the <a href="/wiki/Yangochiroptera" title="Yangochiroptera">Yangochiroptera</a> and at least once in the horseshoe bats (Rhinolophidae):<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<p>The second proposes that laryngeal echolocation had a single origin in Chiroptera, i.e. that it was <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> to the group, and was subsequently lost in the family <a href="/wiki/Pteropodidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Pteropodidae">Pteropodidae</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jebb&#95;Huang&#95;Pippel&#95;2020&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jebb_Huang_Pippel_2020-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Later, the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Rousettus" title="Rousettus">Rousettus</a></i> in the Pteropodidae family evolved a different mechanism of echolocation using a system of tongue-clicking:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Springer&#95;Teeling&#95;Madsen&#95;2001&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Springer_Teeling_Madsen_2001-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Calls_and_ecology">Calls and ecology</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Calls and ecology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Echolocating bats occupy a diverse set of ecological conditions; they can be found living in environments as different as <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, and hunting for food sources as different as insects, frogs, nectar, fruit, and blood. The characteristics of an echolocation call are adapted to the particular environment, hunting behavior, and food source of the particular bat. The adaptation of echolocation calls to ecological factors is constrained by the phylogenetic relationship of the bats, leading to a process known as descent with modification, and resulting in the diversity of the Chiroptera today.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jones&#95;2006&#95;29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_2006-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fenton&#95;1995&#95;32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fenton_1995-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Simmons&#95;1980&#95;31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simmons_1980-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bats can inadvertently jam each other, and in some situations they may stop calling to avoid jamming.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chiu&#95;Xian&#95;2008&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiu_Xian_2008-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Flying insects are a common source of food for echolocating bats and some insects (moths in particular) can hear the calls of predatory bats. However the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Tympanal_organ" title="Tympanal organ">hearing organs</a> in moths predates the origins of bats, so while many moths do listen for approaching bat echolocation their ears did not originally evolve in response to selective pressures from bats.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These moth adaptations provide <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_pressure" title="Evolutionary pressure">selective pressure</a> for bats to improve their insect-hunting systems and this cycle culminates in a moth-bat "<a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_arms_race" title="Evolutionary arms race">evolutionary arms race</a>".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Neural_mechanisms">Neural mechanisms</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Neural mechanisms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Because bats use echolocation to orient themselves and to locate objects, their auditory systems are adapted for this purpose, highly specialized for sensing and interpreting the stereotyped echolocation calls characteristic of their own species. This specialization is evident from the inner ear up to the highest levels of information processing in the auditory cortex.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading5"><h5 id="Inner_ear_and_primary_sensory_neurons">Inner ear and primary sensory neurons</h5><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Inner ear and primary sensory neurons"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Both CF and FM bats have specialized inner ears which allow them to hear sounds in the ultrasonic range, far outside the range of human hearing. Although in most other aspects, the bat's auditory organs are similar to those of most other mammals, certain bats (<a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_bats" class="mw-redirect" title="Horseshoe bats">horseshoe bats</a>, <i>Rhinolophus spp.</i> and the <a href="/wiki/Moustached_bat" class="mw-redirect" title="Moustached bat">moustached bat</a>, <i>Pteronotus parnelii</i>) with a constant frequency (CF) component to their call (known as high duty cycle bats) do have a few additional adaptations for detecting the predominant frequency (and harmonics) of the CF vocalization. These include a narrow frequency "tuning" of the inner ear organs, with an especially large area responding to the frequency of the bat's returning echoes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Neuweiler&#95;2003&#95;33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neuweiler_2003-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Basilar_membrane" title="Basilar membrane">basilar membrane</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Cochlea" title="Cochlea">cochlea</a> contains the first of these specializations for echo information processing. In bats that use CF signals, the section of the membrane that responds to the frequency of returning echoes is much larger than the region of response for any other frequency. For example, in the greater horseshoe bat, <i><a href="/wiki/Rhinolophus_ferrumequinum" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhinolophus ferrumequinum">Rhinolophus ferrumequinum</a></i>, there is a disproportionately lengthened and thickened section of the membrane that responds to sounds around 83&#160;kHz, the constant frequency of the echo produced by the bat's call. This area of high sensitivity to a specific, narrow range of frequency is known as an "<a href="/wiki/Acoustic_fovea" class="mw-redirect" title="Acoustic fovea">acoustic fovea</a>".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Echolocating bats have cochlear hairs that are especially resistant to intense noise. Cochlear hair cells are essential for hearing sensitivity, and can be damaged by intense noise. As bats are regularly exposed to intense noise through echolocation, resistance to degradation by intense noise is necessary.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Further along the auditory pathway, the movement of the basilar membrane results in the stimulation of primary auditory neurons. Many of these neurons are specifically "tuned" (respond most strongly) to the narrow frequency range of returning echoes of CF calls. Because of the large size of the acoustic fovea, the number of neurons responding to this region, and thus to the echo frequency, is especially high.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Carew&#95;2001&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carew_2001-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading5"><h5 id="Inferior_colliculus">Inferior colliculus</h5><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Inferior colliculus"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Inferior_colliculus" title="Inferior colliculus">Inferior colliculus</a>, a structure in the bat's midbrain, information from lower in the auditory processing pathway is integrated and sent on to the auditory cortex. As George Pollak and others showed in a series of papers in 1977, the <a href="/wiki/Interneurons" class="mw-redirect" title="Interneurons">interneurons</a> in this region have a very high level of sensitivity to time differences, since the time delay between a call and the returning echo tells the bat its distance from the target object. While most neurons respond more quickly to stronger stimuli, collicular neurons maintain their timing accuracy even as signal intensity changes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pollak&#95;Marsh&#95;1977&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pollak_Marsh_1977-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These interneurons are specialized for time sensitivity in several ways. First, when activated, they generally respond with only one or two <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">action potentials</a>. This short duration of response allows their action potentials to give a specific indication of the moment when the stimulus arrived, and to respond accurately to stimuli that occur close in time to one another. The neurons have a very low threshold of activation – they respond quickly even to weak stimuli. Finally, for FM signals, each interneuron is tuned to a specific frequency within the sweep, as well as to that same frequency in the following echo. There is specialization for the CF component of the call at this level as well. The high proportion of neurons responding to the frequency of the acoustic fovea actually increases at this level.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pollak&#95;Marsh&#95;1977&#95;57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pollak_Marsh_1977-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading5"><h5 id="Auditory_cortex">Auditory cortex</h5><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Auditory cortex"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Auditory_cortex" title="Auditory cortex">auditory cortex</a> in bats is quite large in comparison with other mammals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Various characteristics of sound are processed by different regions of the cortex, each providing different information about the location or movement of a target object. Most of the existing studies on information processing in the auditory cortex of the bat have been done by <a href="/wiki/Nobuo_Suga" title="Nobuo Suga">Nobuo Suga</a> on the mustached bat, <i><a href="/wiki/Pteronotus_parnellii" class="mw-redirect" title="Pteronotus parnellii">Pteronotus parnellii</a></i>. This bat's call has both CF tone and FM sweep components.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suga&#95;1975&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suga_1975-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suga&#95;1987&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suga_1987-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Suga and his colleagues have shown that the cortex contains a series of "maps" of auditory information, each of which is organized systematically based on characteristics of sound such as <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amplitude" title="Amplitude">amplitude</a>. The neurons in these areas respond only to a specific combination of frequency and timing (sound-echo delay), and are known as combination-sensitive neurons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suga&#95;1975&#95;59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suga_1975-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suga&#95;1987&#95;60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suga_1987-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The systematically organized maps in the auditory cortex respond to various aspects of the echo signal, such as its delay and its velocity. These regions are composed of "combination sensitive" neurons that require at least two specific stimuli to elicit a response. The neurons vary systematically across the maps, which are organized by acoustic features of the sound and can be two dimensional. The different features of the call and its echo are used by the bat to determine important characteristics of their prey. The maps include:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suga&#95;1975&#95;59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suga_1975-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suga&#95;1987&#95;60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suga_1987-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bat_Auditory_Cortex.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Bat_Auditory_Cortex.svg/330px-Bat_Auditory_Cortex.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="171" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Bat_Auditory_Cortex.svg/960px-Bat_Auditory_Cortex.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1161" data-file-height="661" /></a><figcaption>Auditory cortex of a bat <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#BA54C0 ; color:black;color:white;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;"><span class="legend-text" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">A</span></span>&#160;FM-FM area </div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#4190B9 ; color:black;color:white;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;"><span class="legend-text" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">B</span></span>&#160;CF-CF area </div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#4AB8B6 ; color:black;color:white;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;"><span class="legend-text" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">C</span></span>&#160;Amplitude-sensitive area </div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#DC7A5B ; color:black;color:white;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;"><span class="legend-text" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">D</span></span>&#160;Frequency-sensitive area </div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#90C675 ; color:black;color:white;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;"><span class="legend-text" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">E</span></span>&#160;DSCF area </div></figcaption></figure>
<ul><li><b>FM-FM area</b>: This region of the cortex contains FM-FM combination-sensitive neurons. These cells respond only to the combination of two FM sweeps: a call and its echo. The neurons in the FM-FM region are often referred to as "delay-tuned", since each responds to a specific time delay between the original call and the echo, in order to find the distance from the target object (the range). Each neuron also shows specificity for one harmonic in the original call and a different harmonic in the echo. The neurons within the FM-FM area of the cortex of <i>Pteronotus</i> are organized into columns, in which the delay time is constant vertically but increases across the horizontal plane. The result is that range is encoded by location on the cortex, and increases systematically across the FM-FM area.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suga&#95;1975&#95;59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suga_1975-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>CF-CF area</b>: Another kind of combination-sensitive neuron is the CF-CF neuron. These respond best to the combination of a CF call containing two given frequencies – a call at 30&#160;kHz (CF1) and one of its additional <a href="/wiki/Harmonics" class="mw-redirect" title="Harmonics">harmonics</a> around 60 or 90&#160;kHz (CF2 or CF3) – and the corresponding echoes. Thus, within the CF-CF region, the changes in echo frequency caused by the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" class="mw-redirect" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> can be compared to the frequency of the original call to calculate the bat's velocity relative to its target object. As in the FM-FM area, information is encoded by its location within the map-like organization of the region. The CF-CF area is first split into the distinct CF1-CF2 and CF1-CF3 areas. Within each area, the CF1 frequency is organized on an axis, perpendicular to the CF2 or CF3 frequency axis. In the resulting grid, each neuron codes for a certain combination of frequencies that is indicative of a specific velocity<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Carew&#95;2001&#95;56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carew_2001-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suga&#95;1975&#95;59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suga_1975-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suga&#95;1987&#95;60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suga_1987-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Doppler shifted constant frequency (DSCF) area</b>: This large section of the cortex is a map of the acoustic fovea, organized by frequency and by amplitude. Neurons in this region respond to CF signals that have been Doppler shifted (in other words, echoes only) and are within the same narrow frequency range to which the acoustic fovea responds. For <i>Pteronotus</i>, this is around 61&#160;kHz. This area is organized into columns, which are arranged radially based on frequency. Within a column, each neuron responds to a specific combination of frequency and amplitude. This brain region is necessary for frequency discrimination.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Carew&#95;2001&#95;56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carew_2001-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suga&#95;1975&#95;59-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suga_1975-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Suga&#95;1987&#95;60-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suga_1987-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Whales">Whales</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Whales"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Toothed_whale_sound_production.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Toothed_whale_sound_production.svg/500px-Toothed_whale_sound_production.svg.png" decoding="async" width="450" height="229" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Toothed_whale_sound_production.svg/960px-Toothed_whale_sound_production.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="260" /></a><figcaption>Diagram illustrating sound generation, propagation and reception in a toothed whale. Outgoing sounds are cyan and incoming ones are green.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Biosonar is valuable to both <a href="/wiki/Toothed_whales" class="mw-redirect" title="Toothed whales">toothed whales</a> (suborder <a href="/wiki/Odontoceti" class="mw-redirect" title="Odontoceti">Odontoceti</a>), including <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porpoise" title="Porpoise">porpoises</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_dolphin" title="River dolphin">river dolphins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Killer_whale" class="mw-redirect" title="Killer whale">killer whales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">sperm whales</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baleen_whales" class="mw-redirect" title="Baleen whales">baleen whales</a> (suborder <a href="/wiki/Mysticeti" class="mw-redirect" title="Mysticeti">Mysticeti</a>), including <a href="/wiki/Right_whales" class="mw-redirect" title="Right whales">right</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bowhead_whales" class="mw-redirect" title="Bowhead whales">bowhead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_right_whales" class="mw-redirect" title="Pygmy right whales">pygmy right</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gray_whales" class="mw-redirect" title="Gray whales">gray whales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rorquals" class="mw-redirect" title="Rorquals">rorquals</a>, because they live in an underwater habitat that has favourable acoustic characteristics and where <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">vision</a> is often extremely limited in range due to absorption or <a href="/wiki/Turbidity" title="Turbidity">turbidity</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Odontocetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Odontocetes">Odontocetes</a> are generally able to hear sounds at <a href="/wiki/Ultrasonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultrasonic">ultrasonic</a> frequencies while <a href="/wiki/Mysticetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Mysticetes">mysticetes</a> hear sounds within the <a href="/wiki/Infrasonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Infrasonic">infrasonic</a> frequency regime.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Viglino&#95;2021&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Viglino_2021-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Whale_evolution">Whale evolution</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Whale evolution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cetacea" class="mw-redirect" title="Cetacea">Cetacean</a> evolution consisted of three main <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_radiation" title="Evolutionary radiation">radiations</a>. Throughout the middle and late <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> periods (49–31.5 million years ago), <a href="/wiki/Archaeocete" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaeocete">archaeocetes</a>, primitive toothed Cetacea that arose from terrestrial mammals, were the only cetaceans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gatesy&#95;Geisler&#95;Chang&#95;2012&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gatesy_Geisler_Chang_2012-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fordyce&#95;1980&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fordyce_1980-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They did not echolocate, but had slightly adapted underwater hearing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fordyce&#95;2003&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fordyce_2003-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By the late middle Eocene, acoustically isolated ear bones had evolved to give <a href="/wiki/Basilosauridae" title="Basilosauridae">basilosaurid</a> archaeocetes directional underwater hearing at low to mid frequencies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lindberg&#95;2007&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindberg_2007-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With the extinction of archaeocetes at the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> (33.9–23 million years ago), two new lineages evolved in a second radiation. Early mysticetes (baleen whales) and odontocetes appeared in the middle Oligocene in New Zealand.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fordyce&#95;1980&#95;65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fordyce_1980-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Extant odontocetes are <a href="/wiki/Monophyletic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> (a single evolutionary group), but echolocation evolved twice, convergently: once in <i><a href="/wiki/Xenorophus" title="Xenorophus">Xenorophus</a></i>, an Oligocene <a href="/wiki/Stem-group" class="mw-redirect" title="Stem-group">stem</a> odontocete, and once in the <a href="/wiki/Crown_group" title="Crown group">crown</a> odontocetes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Racicot&#95;2019&#95;36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Racicot_2019-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<td class="clade-label"><i><b>directional&#160;u/water&#160;hearing</b></i>

<td rowspan="2" class="clade-leaf">
<p><a href="/wiki/Basilosauridae" title="Basilosauridae">Basilosauridae</a> †
</p>
</td></tr>

<td class="clade-slabel last"><i>mid/late&#160;<a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a></i>
