<td class="clade-slabel last">
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<table style="font-size: 10px; background: whitesmoke; float:right;">
<caption style="font-size:12px;"><b>Cetacean evolution timeline</b><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fordyce&#95;1980&#95;65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fordyce_1980-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr style="background:lightgray;">
<th scope="col" style="width: 50px;">Epoch
</th>
<th scope="col" style="width: 55px;">Start date
</th>
<th scope="col" style="width: 100px;">Event
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a></td>
<td>23 <a href="/wiki/Myr" class="mw-redirect" title="Myr">mya</a></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">Adaptive radiation</a>, esp. of dolphins
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a></td>
<td>34 mya</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Odontocetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Odontocetes">Odontocetes</a> echolocation
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a></td>
<td>49 mya</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Archaeoceti" title="Archaeoceti">Archaeocetes</a> underwater hearing
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Physical restructuring of the oceans has played a role in the evolution of echolocation. Global cooling at the <a href="/wiki/Eocene-Oligocene_boundary" class="mw-redirect" title="Eocene-Oligocene boundary">Eocene-Oligocene boundary</a> caused a change from a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_and_icehouse_Earth" title="Greenhouse and icehouse Earth">greenhouse to an icehouse world</a>. Tectonic openings created the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a> with a free flowing <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current" title="Antarctic Circumpolar Current">Antarctic Circumpolar Current</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fordyce&#95;2003&#95;66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fordyce_2003-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lindberg&#95;2007&#95;67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindberg_2007-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Steeman&#95;2009&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steeman_2009-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These events encouraged selection for the ability to locate and capture prey in turbid river waters, which enabled the odontocetes to invade and feed at depths below the <a href="/wiki/Photic_zone" title="Photic zone">photic zone</a>. In particular, echolocation below the photic zone could have been a predation adaptation to <a href="/wiki/Diel_vertical_migration" title="Diel vertical migration">diel migrating</a> <a href="/wiki/Cephalopods" class="mw-redirect" title="Cephalopods">cephalopods</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lindberg&#95;2007&#95;67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindberg_2007-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The family <a href="/wiki/Delphinidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Delphinidae">Delphinidae</a> (dolphins) diversified in the <a href="/wiki/Neogene" title="Neogene">Neogene</a> (23–2.6 million years ago), evolving extremely specialized echolocation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fordyce&#95;2003&#95;66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fordyce_2003-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Four proteins play a major role in toothed whale echolocation. <a href="/wiki/Prestin" title="Prestin">Prestin</a>, a motor protein of the outer hair cells of the inner ear of the mammalian <a href="/wiki/Cochlea" title="Cochlea">cochlea</a>, is associated with hearing sensitivity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Liu&#95;Rossiter&#95;Xiuqun&#95;2010&#95;71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liu_Rossiter_Xiuqun_2010-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has undergone two clear episodes of accelerated evolution in cetaceans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Liu&#95;Rossiter&#95;Xiuqun&#95;2010&#95;71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liu_Rossiter_Xiuqun_2010-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first is connected to odontocete divergence, when echolocation first developed, and the second with the increase in echolocation frequency among dolphins. <a href="/wiki/TMC1" title="TMC1">Tmc1</a> and Pjvk are proteins related to hearing sensitivity: Tmc1 is associated with hair cell development and high-frequency hearing, and Pjvk  with hair cell function.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Davies&#95;Cotton&#95;Kirwan&#95;2012&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies_Cotton_Kirwan_2012-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Molecular_evolution" title="Molecular evolution">Molecular evolution</a> of Tmc1 and Pjvk indicates positive selection for echolocation in odontocetes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Davies&#95;Cotton&#95;Kirwan&#95;2012&#95;72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies_Cotton_Kirwan_2012-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/CLDN14" title="CLDN14">Cldn14</a>, a member of the tight junction proteins which form barriers between inner ear cells, shows the same evolutionary pattern as Prestin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The two events of protein evolution, for Prestin and Cldn14, occurred at the same times as the tectonic opening of the <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a> (34–31 Ma) and Antarctic ice growth at the Middle <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> climate transition (14 Ma), with the divergence of odontocetes and mysticetes occurring with the former, and the speciation of Delphinidae with the latter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Steeman&#95;2009&#95;68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steeman_2009-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The evolution of two cranial structures may be linked to echolocation. Cranial telescoping (overlap between <a href="/wiki/Frontal_bone" title="Frontal bone">frontal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maxilla" title="Maxilla">maxillary</a> bones, and rearwards displacement of the nostrils<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Roston&#95;Roth&#95;2019&#95;74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roston_Roth_2019-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>) developed first in <a href="/wiki/Xenorophidae" title="Xenorophidae">xenorophids</a>. It evolved further in stem odontocetes, arriving at full cranial telescoping in the crown odontocetes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Churchill&#95;Geisler&#95;Beatty&#95;2018&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Churchill_Geisler_Beatty_2018-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Movement of the nostrils may have allowed for a larger nasal apparatus and <a href="/wiki/Melon_(cetacean)" title="Melon (cetacean)">melon</a> for echolocation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Churchill&#95;Geisler&#95;Beatty&#95;2018&#95;75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Churchill_Geisler_Beatty_2018-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This change occurred after the divergence of the neocetes from the basilosaurids.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Coombs&#95;Clavel&#95;Park&#95;2020&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coombs_Clavel_Park_2020-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first shift towards cranial asymmetry occurred in the Early Oligocene, prior to the xenorophids.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Coombs&#95;Clavel&#95;Park&#95;2020&#95;76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coombs_Clavel_Park_2020-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A xenorophid fossil (<i>Cotylocara macei</i>) has cranial asymmetry, and shows other indicators of echolocation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geisler&#95;Colbert&#95;Carew&#95;2014&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geisler_Colbert_Carew_2014-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, basal xenorophids lack cranial asymmetry, indicating that this likely evolved twice.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Coombs&#95;Clavel&#95;Park&#95;2020&#95;76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coombs_Clavel_Park_2020-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Extant odontocetes have asymmetric nasofacial regions; generally, the <a href="/wiki/Median_plane" title="Median plane">median plane</a> is shifted to the left and structures on the right are larger.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geisler&#95;Colbert&#95;Carew&#95;2014&#95;77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geisler_Colbert_Carew_2014-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Both cranial telescoping and asymmetry likely relate to sound production for echolocation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Churchill&#95;Geisler&#95;Beatty&#95;2018&#95;75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Churchill_Geisler_Beatty_2018-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Mechanism">Mechanism</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mechanism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><audio id="mwe_player_3" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" style="width:250px;" data-durationhint="86" data-mwtitle="Killer_whale_residents_broadband.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Killer_whale_residents_broadband.ogg"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Killer_whale_residents_broadband.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/eb/Killer_whale_residents_broadband.ogg/Killer_whale_residents_broadband.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span><figcaption>Southern Alaskan resident <a href="/wiki/Killer_whale" class="mw-redirect" title="Killer whale">killer whales</a> using echolocation</figcaption></figure>
<p>Thirteen species of extant odontocetes <a href="/wiki/Convergently_evolved" class="mw-redirect" title="Convergently evolved">convergently evolved</a> narrow-band high-frequency (NBHF) echolocation in four separate events. These species include the families <a href="/wiki/Kogiidae" title="Kogiidae">Kogiidae</a> (pygmy sperm whales) and <a href="/wiki/Phocoenidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Phocoenidae">Phocoenidae</a> (porpoises), as well as some species of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Lagenorhynchus" title="Lagenorhynchus">Lagenorhynchus</a></i>, all of <i><a href="/wiki/Cephalorhynchus" title="Cephalorhynchus">Cephalorhynchus</a></i>, and the <a href="/wiki/La_Plata_dolphin" title="La Plata dolphin">La Plata dolphin</a>. NBHF is thought to have evolved as a means of predator evasion; NBHF-producing species are small relative to other odontocetes, making them viable prey to large species such as the <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orca</a>. However, because three of the groups developed NBHF prior to the emergence of the orca, predation by other ancient raptorial odontocetes must have been the driving force for the development of NBHF, not predation by the orca. Orcas, and, presumably ancient raptorial odontocetes such as <i>Acrophyseter</i>, are unable to hear frequencies above 100&#160;kHz.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another reason for variation in echolocation is habitat. For all sonar systems, the limiting factor deciding whether a returning echo is detected is the echo-to-noise ratio (ENR). The ENR is given by the emitted source level (SL) plus the target strength, minus the two-way transmission loss (absorption and spreading) and the received noise.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kyhn,&#95;L.A.&#95;2010&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kyhn,_L.A._2010-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Animals will adapt either to maximize range under noise-limited conditions (increase source level) or to reduce noise clutter in a shallow and/or littered habitat (decrease source level). In cluttered habitats, such as coastal areas, prey ranges are smaller, and species such as <a href="/wiki/Commerson%27s_dolphin" title="Commerson&#39;s dolphin">Commerson's dolphin</a> (<i>Cephalorhynchus commersonii</i>) have lowered source levels to better suit their environment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kyhn,&#95;L.A.&#95;2010&#95;79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kyhn,_L.A._2010-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Mechanics&#95;of&#95;echolocation&#95;in&#95;whales"></span>
</p><p>Toothed whales emit a focused beam of high-frequency clicks in the direction that their head is pointing. Sounds are generated by passing air from the bony nares through the <a href="/wiki/Whale_vocalization#Odontocete_whales" title="Whale vocalization">phonic lips</a>. These sounds are reflected by the dense concave bone of the cranium and an air sac at its base. The focused beam is modulated by a large fatty organ known as the melon. This acts like an acoustic lens because it is composed of lipids of differing densities. Most toothed whales use clicks in a series, or click train, for echolocation, while the sperm whale may produce clicks individually. Toothed whale whistles do not appear to be used in echolocation. Different rates of click production in a click train give rise to the familiar barks, squeals and growls of the <a href="/wiki/Bottlenose_dolphin" title="Bottlenose dolphin">bottlenose dolphin</a>. A click train with a repetition rate over 600 per second is called a burst pulse. In bottlenose dolphins, the auditory brain response resolves individual clicks up to 600 per second, but yields a graded response for higher repetition rates.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>It has been suggested that the arrangement of the teeth of some smaller toothed whales may be an adaptation for echolocation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The teeth of a bottlenose dolphin, for example, are not arranged symmetrically when seen from a vertical plane. This asymmetry could possibly be an aid in sensing if echoes from its biosonar are coming from one side or the other; but this has not been tested experimentally.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Echoes are received using complex fatty structures around the lower jaw as the primary reception path, from where they are transmitted to the middle ear via a continuous fat body. Lateral sound may be received through fatty lobes surrounding the ears with a similar density to water. Some researchers believe that when they approach the object of interest, they protect themselves against the louder echo by quietening the emitted sound. In bats this is known to happen, but here the hearing sensitivity is also reduced close to a target.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Oilbirds_and_swiftlets">Oilbirds and swiftlets</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Oilbirds and swiftlets"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Palawan_swiftlet_(Aerodramus_palawanensis)_hunting_by_echolocation.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Palawan_swiftlet_%28Aerodramus_palawanensis%29_hunting_by_echolocation.JPG/250px-Palawan_swiftlet_%28Aerodramus_palawanensis%29_hunting_by_echolocation.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Palawan_swiftlet_%28Aerodramus_palawanensis%29_hunting_by_echolocation.JPG/500px-Palawan_swiftlet_%28Aerodramus_palawanensis%29_hunting_by_echolocation.JPG 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a><figcaption>A Palawan swiftlet (<i>Aerodramus palawanensis</i>) flies in complete darkness inside the Puerto Princesa subterranean river cave.</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Oilbird" title="Oilbird">Oilbirds</a> and some species of <a href="/wiki/Swiftlet" title="Swiftlet">swiftlet</a> are known to use a relatively crude form of echolocation compared to that of bats and dolphins. These nocturnal birds emit calls while flying and use the calls to navigate through trees and caves where they live.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Terrestrial_mammals">Terrestrial mammals</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Terrestrial mammals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Shrews#Echolocation" class="mw-redirect" title="Shrews">Shrews §&#160;Echolocation</a></div>
<p>Terrestrial mammals other than bats known or thought to echolocate include <a href="/wiki/Shrew" title="Shrew">shrews</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Siemers&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siemers-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Tenrec" title="Tenrec">tenrecs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_pygmy_dormouse" title="Chinese pygmy dormouse">Chinese pygmy dormice</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Solenodon" title="Solenodon">solenodons</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Eisenberg1966&#95;92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eisenberg1966-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Shrew sounds, unlike those of bats, are low amplitude, broadband, multi-harmonic and frequency modulated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Siemers&#95;89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siemers-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They contain no echolocation clicks with reverberations, and appear to be used for simple, close range spatial orientation. In contrast to bats, shrews use echolocation only to investigate their habitat rather than to pinpoint food.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Siemers&#95;89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siemers-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is evidence that blinded <a href="/wiki/Laboratory_rats" class="mw-redirect" title="Laboratory rats">laboratory rats</a> can use echolocation to navigate mazes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Humans">Humans</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Humans"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_echolocation" title="Human echolocation">Human echolocation</a></div>
<p>Some humans, particularly, blind people, have developed the skill to produce brief sounds, using their mouth, hands or other objects, and listen to the echo. This technique allows them to locate larger objects in their vicinity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-&#80;MID&#95;27538733&#95;94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PMID_27538733-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Countermeasures">Countermeasures</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_echolocation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Countermeasures"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Argema_mimosae_male.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Argema_mimosae_male.jpg/250px-Argema_mimosae_male.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="253" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Argema_mimosae_male.jpg/500px-Argema_mimosae_male.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2626" data-file-height="3500" /></a><figcaption>The especially long tails on the hindwings of the <a href="/wiki/Argema_mimosae" title="Argema mimosae">African moon moth</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Saturniidae" title="Saturniidae">Saturniid</a>, oscillate in flight, deflecting the hunting bat's attack to the tails and thus enabling the moth to evade capture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rubin&#95;Hamilton&#95;2018&#95;95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin_Hamilton_2018-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Some insects that are predated by bats have <a href="/wiki/Anti-predator_adaptation" title="Anti-predator adaptation">anti-predator adaptations</a>,  including predator avoidance,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Spangler&#95;1988&#95;96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spangler_1988-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> attack deflection,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rubin&#95;Hamilton&#95;2018&#95;95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin_Hamilton_2018-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and ultrasonic clicks which appear to function as warnings rather than <a href="/wiki/Echolocation_jamming" title="Echolocation jamming">echolocation jamming</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chiu&#95;Xian&#95;2008&#95;49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiu_Xian_2008-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Phillips&#95;2006&#95;97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips_2006-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Tiger moths (<a href="/wiki/Arctiidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctiidae">Arctiidae</a>) of different species (two thirds of the species tested) respond to simulated attack by echolocating bats by producing an accelerating series of clicks. The species <i><a href="/wiki/Bertholdia_trigona" title="Bertholdia trigona">Bertholdia trigona</a></i> has been shown to jam bat echolocation: when pit against naïve big brown bats, ultrasound was immediately and consistently effective at preventing bat attack. Bats came in contact with silent control moths 400% more often than with <i>B. trigona</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Moth ultrasound can also function to <a href="/wiki/Deimatic_behaviour" title="Deimatic behaviour">startle</a> the bat (a bluffing tactic), warn the bat that the moth is distasteful (honest signalling, <a href="/wiki/Aposematism" title="Aposematism">aposematism</a>), or mimic chemically defended species. Both aposematism and mimicry have been shown to confer a survival advantage against bat attack.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The greater wax moth (<i><a href="/wiki/Galleria_mellonella" title="Galleria mellonella">Galleria mellonella</a></i>) takes predator avoidance actions such as dropping, looping, and freezing when it detects ultrasound waves, indicating that it can both detect and differentiate between ultrasound frequencies used by predators and signals from other members of their species.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Spangler&#95;1988&#95;96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spangler_1988-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some members of the <i><a href="/wiki/Saturniidae" title="Saturniidae">Saturniidae</a></i> moth family, which includes giant silk moths, have long tails on the hindwings, especially those in the Attacini and <a href="/wiki/Arsenurinae" title="Arsenurinae">Arsenurinae</a> subgroups. The tails oscillate in flight, creating echoes which deflect the hunting bat's attack from the moth's body to the tails. The species <i><a href="/wiki/Argema_mimosae" title="Argema mimosae">Argema mimosae</a></i> (the African moon moth), which has especially long tails, was the most likely to evade capture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rubin&#95;Hamilton&#95;2018&#95;95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin_Hamilton_2018-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite&#95;note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFDijkgraaf1960" class="citation journal cs1">Dijkgraaf, Sven (March 1960).
